A Dream Confirms a Creed Poem: Al-Farawi's Biography and a Prophetic Vision
Ibn Asakir records a dream where the Prophet approves a creed poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream vision of the Prophet was used to validate a creed poem in Islamic tradition, highlighting the role of such visions in scholarly authentication.

Core Idea
A dream vision of the Prophet validates al-Qushayri's creed poem.

This lesson presents a remarkable account from Ibn Asakir's biographical work. A revered scholar's son recites a creed poem before the Prophet in a dream, and the Prophet corrects a line, underscoring the importance of precision in transmitting creed. The story illustrates how dreams were sometimes cited to support theological works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the chain of narration and the dream narrative.
The passage ends abruptly in the original manuscript.
2
Identify the narrators
Trace the chain of transmission from Ibn Asakir back to the dreamer, al-Simjuri. This chain (isnad) shows how the account was preserved.
The chain is: Ibn Asakir ← al-Tabasi ← handwriting of al-Sa'idi ← al-Simjuri.
3
Analyze the dream content
Focus on the dream: the Prophet is sitting with 'Aisha, and al-Farawi's son Muhammad recites al-Qushayri's creed poem. The Prophet corrects a mistake in recitation.
The correction shows the Prophet's attention to precise wording.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why such a dream was recorded: it serves to affirm the correctness of al-Qushayri's poem, which is an Ash'ari creed text. Dreams of the Prophet were sometimes used to endorse theological works.
This reflects the broader practice of citing dreams for validation.
5
Discuss the role of dreams in Islamic scholarship
Think about how dreams are viewed in Islam: they can be a source of inspiration but not binding authority. This narrative emphasizes the Prophet's role as a teacher even in dreams.
The dream is presented as a historical account, not a legal proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم شيخنا الإمام أبو عبد الله الفراوي النيسابوري رحمه الله حدثنى الشيخ أبو المحاسن عبد الرزاق بن محمد بن أبي نصر الطبسي بنيسابور قال وجدت بخط الإمام أبي مسعود الفضل ، بن أحمد الصاعدي قال حكى لى الأمير أبو الحسن على بن الحسن السيمجوری القاینی رحمه يوم السبت سلخ رجب ، عظم الله بركته سنة ثلاث وخمسين وأربعمائة قال أني كنت أول من أمس بين النائم واليقظان فرأيت كأنك حضرت عندي وقلت لي أن الصوفية جعلوا ولدك محمداً نائبهم في عقد المجلس فكما سمعت منك هذا المقال رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جالساً كبشبه المتكيء حاسراً عن رأسه وبجنبه شخص علمت أنه عائشة رضى عنها ثم أن ولدك أنشد بين يدى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم القصيدة المنظومة في الاعتقاد التي مفتتحها : بحمد الله أفتتح المقالا وقد جلت أياديه تعالى من إنشاد الأستاذ الإمام أبى القسم عبد الكريم بن هوازن القشيري ، ثم أنه جرى على لسان ولدك محمد في أثناء إنشاده بين يدى رسول الله الله عليه وسلم هذه القصيدة شيء ، فقال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كيف قلت كالمستدرك عليه فرجع إلى أبيات قبلها فأنشدها بين يدى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مع البيت المنتهى إليه فأنشدها على
And among them is our teacher, the Imam Abu `Abd Allah al-Farawi al-Naysaburi, may Allah have mercy on him. The teacher Abu al-Mahasin `Abd al-Razzaq ibn Muhammad ibn Abi Nasr al-Tabasi in Nishapur narrated to me, saying: I found in the handwriting of the Imam Abu Mas`ud al-Fadl ibn Ahmad al-Sa`idi, who said: The prince Abu al-Hasan `Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Simjuri al-Qa'ini, may Allah have mercy on him, narrated to me on Saturday the end of Rajab, may Allah magnify his blessing, of the year 453 AH, who said: I was between sleep and wakefulness at the beginning of last night when I saw as if you came to me and said to me that the Sufis had made your son Muhammad their deputy to preside over the gathering. As soon as I heard this statement from you, I saw the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, sitting as if reclining, with his head uncovered, and beside him a person I knew to be `A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her. Then your son recited before the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, the poem composed on creed, which opens with 'With the praise of Allah I begin the discourse, and His favors are exalted, exalted is He,' from the recitation of the master, the Imam Abu al-Qasim `Abd al-Karim ibn Hawazin al-Qushayri. Then something happened to your son's tongue during his recitation before the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, of this poem. The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said to him, 'How did you say?' as if correcting him. So he went back to verses before it and recited them before the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, with the verse that followed, and he recited them upon...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Abu `Abd Allah al-Farawi, but it consists almost entirely of a narrated dream vision (ru'ya) that occurred to the prince al-Simjuri. In the dream, the prince sees the Prophet Muhammad sitting with `A'isha, and al-Farawi's son Muhammad recites a creed poem by al-Qushayri before the Prophet. The Prophet corrects a recitation error, indicating divine approval of the poem but also meticulousness in its recitation. The dream underscores the authority and acceptability of al-Qushayri's creed poem, which was a standard Ash'ari text. The narration chain is given: from al-Tabasi from the handwriting of al-Sa'idi from al-Simjuri. The passage ends abruptly mid-sentence on the page.

Key Terms
رؤيا
اعتقاد
القصيدة المنظومة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think dreams have no value in Islamic scholarship.
Correction: While dreams are not a source of law, they can serve as supportive evidence for the correctness of existing texts, as this account shows.

B) The prince al-Simjuri
C) Al-Farawi's son Muhammad
D) Abu al-Mahasin al-Tabasi

Key Takeaway
A dream vision of the Prophet was recorded to affirm the accuracy of al-Qushayri's creed poem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet Muhammad appeared to you tonight and corrected a mistake in your belief about Allah, would you be ready to accept it with the same humility as the young reciter? Or would you argue or feel defensive?
?
The Sufis made the son a deputy to lead the gathering. In your own life, who is delegating authority to you, and are you fulfilling it with the same care for correctness that the Prophet demanded?
?
This dream was recorded and passed down as a validation of a creed poem. What is the creed that you truly hold in your heart, and would you be willing to have it examined by the Prophet? If not, what must change today?

Biography of a Judge and Scholar
Ibn Asakir records the biography of a qadi and scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and career of a medieval Islamic judge and scholar as recorded by Ibn 'Asakir.

Core Idea
Biographical entries are concise yet packed with information about education, career, and reputation.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's *Tabyin* presents the biography of a scholar and judge, likely al-Qadi Abu al-'Abbas. It follows a standard pattern: lineage, travels, studies, appointments, and death. As you read, notice how the author selects specific details to convey the scholar's prestige and expertise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Pay attention to the chain of offices mentioned.
2
Identify the stages of the scholar's life
The biography moves from early asceticism to rising prominence, then lists his official positions. Notice the contrast.
3
Examine the reported praise
A fellow scholar says the qadi's speech overcame that of another imam. This shows his debating skill and reputation.
This is a key detail about his scholarly standing.
4
Consider the overall purpose
Ask: why would Ibn 'Asakir include this biography? What does it tell us about the scholar's legacy?
Biographies in such works serve to preserve the memory of notable figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بن ثابت بن سبع إسحق، ورحل إلى نيسابور وتفقه بالإمام أبي بكر محمد الحسين الخجندى مدرس النظامية بأصبهان، وسمع بها شيئاً من الحديث ورجع إلى العراق وكان يتزهد فى ابتداء أمره ثم تقدم عند الخلفاء وولى قضاء نهر معلا ببغداد والحسبة والنظر فى الوقوف وفى أمر ترب الخلفاء والصلاة بأمير المؤمنين المسترشد بالله رحمه الله وتأديب ولده أبي جعفر المنصور الراشد بالله وكان مقدماً فى المعرفة بالمذهب والخلاف ، حسن المناظرة حلو العبارة . سمعت الشيخ أبا عبد الله المقدسى وقال له بعض الفقهاء لقد ظهر اليوم كلام القاضى أبى العباس على كلام الشيخ الإمام أسعد ومتى لم يظهر . كلام القاضي على كلامه ، مات ليلة الاثنين مستهل رجب سنة وعشرين وخمساية ودفن في تربة الشيخ أبي إسحق الشيرازي كتب إلى بذلك أبو المعمر
Ibn Thabit Ibn Sab' Ishaq, and he traveled to Nishapur and studied fiqh under Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad al-Husayn al-Khujandi, teacher of the Nizamiyya in Isfahan, and heard there some hadith and returned to Iraq. He was ascetic at the beginning of his affair, then he advanced before the caliphs and was appointed judge of Nahr Ma'la in Baghdad, and [over] the hisba and supervision of endowments and the affairs of the tombs of the caliphs, and leading the prayer for the Commander of the Faithful al-Mustarshid billah, may Allah have mercy on him, and teaching his son Abu Ja'far al-Mansur al-Rashid billah. He was advanced in knowledge of the madhhab and disagreement [among jurists], good at debate, sweet in expression. I heard Shaykh Abu 'Abd Allah al-Maqdisi and some fuqaha' said to him: 'Today the speech of al-Qadi Abu al-'Abbas has appeared over the speech of the Shaykh al-Imam As'ad, and when it does not appear, the speech of the qadi [overcomes] his speech.' He died on Monday night at the beginning of Rajab of the year five hundred and twenty, and was buried in the tomb of Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi. Abu al-Mu'ammar wrote to me about that.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice of a scholar, likely al-Qadi Abu al-'Abbas (though his full name is obscured by OCR). Ibn 'Asakir begins by stating his lineage and early studies: he traveled to Nishapur to study fiqh under a famous teacher, heard hadith, and returned to Iraq. He started as an ascetic but later gained prominence with the caliphs, holding multiple judicial and administrative posts: judge, market inspector, overseer of endowments and caliphal tombs, and leading prayer for the caliph al-Mustarshid. He also tutored al-Mustarshid's son. The author then notes his expertise in the madhhab and legal disagreement, praising his debating skill and eloquence. A reported statement from a scholar confirms that the qadi's argument prevailed over that of another imam. Finally, we are given his death date and burial place. This notice follows the pattern of traditional biographical entries: name, education, career, reputation, death.

Misconception: The biography describes a theologian or Sufi saint.
Correction: The subject was a jurist (faqih) and judge, not primarily a theologian or mystic, as evidenced by his positions and expertise in madhhab and khilaf.

Dream of the Prophet Approving Qushayri's Creed Poem
A dream narrative validating Qushayri's creed poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream vision of the Prophet validated a creed poem and elevated its status.

Core Idea
A prophetic dream confirms a Sufi creed poem as orthodox.

This lesson presents a remarkable account from Ibn 'Asakir's biographical work. A dream vision is reported where the Prophet not only approves a creed poem by al-Qushayri but also appoints a young boy as his deputy for a session. This narrative serves to authenticate the poem's content and the status of its reciter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the chain of transmission and the dream details.
The passage has high OCR risk; some words may be uncertain.
2
Identify key figures and events
List the narrators (al-Farawi, al-Tabasi, etc.), the dreamer (al-Simjuri), the boy (Muhammad al-Farawi), and the Prophet. Note the sequence: appearance of Prophet, recitation, correction, appointment, Fatima's arrival, white clothes.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this dream was recorded and how it functioned to validate the poem and the Sufi tradition. Consider the role of dreams in Islamic piety.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم شيخنا الإمام أبو عبد الله الفراوي النيسابوري رحمه الله حدثنى الشيخ أبو المحاسن عبد الرزاق بن محمد بن أبي نصر الطبسي بنيسابور قال وجدت بخط الإمام أبي مسعود الفضل بن أحمد الصاعدي قال حكى لى الأمير أبو الحسن على بن الحسن السيمجوری القاینی رحمه يوم السبت سلخ رجب عظم الله بركته سنة ثلاث وخمسين وأربعمائة قال أني كنت أول من أمس بين النائم واليقظان فرأيت كأنك حضرت عندي وقلت لي أن الصوفية جعلوا ولدك محمداً نائبهم في عقد المجلس فكما سمعت منك هذا المقال رأيت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جالساً كبشبه المتكيء حاسراً عن رأسه وبجنبه شخص علمت أنه عائشة رضى عنها ثم أن ولدك أنشد بين يدى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم القصيدة المنظومة في الاعتقاد التي مفتتحها : بحمد الله أفتتح المقالا وقد جلت أياديه تعالى من إنشاد الأستاذ الإمام أبى القسم عبد الكريم بن هوازن القشيري ، ثم أنه جرى على لسان ولدك محمد في أثناء إنشاده بين يدى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم هذه القصيدة شيء ، فقال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كيف قلت كالمستدرك عليه فرجع إلى أبيات قبلها فأنشدها بين يدى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مع البيت المنتهى إليه فأنشدها على ما رضى عليه السلام إلى أن فرغ من إنشاد تمام القصيدة ثم قال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جعلتك نائبى فى عقد المجلس ثم في الحال جاءت فاطمة عليها السلام وجلست بين رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وعائشة فقال لها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يغرمها يعنى على ما بينها وبين فاتها بعد قيامها من جنبه حال إنشاد هذا الصبى ورأيت على ولدك في تلك الحالة ثيابا بيضا ثم ذكر الأمير أبو الحسن السيمجوري هذه الرؤيا بين يدى جماعة المتصوفة بنيسابور في خانقاه الشيخ أبي عبد الرحمن السلمي فكلهم أعجبوا بهذه البشارة ، توفى الأمير أبو الحسن رحمه الله في شهر رمضان سنة ثلاث وخمسين وأربعمائة
And among them is our shaykh, the Imam Abu 'Abd Allah al-Farawi al-Naysaburi, may Allah have mercy on him. The shaykh Abu al-Mahasin 'Abd al-Razzaq ibn Muhammad ibn Abi Nasr al-Tabasi narrated to me in Nishapur, saying: I found in the handwriting of the Imam Abu Mas'ud al-Fadl ibn Ahmad al-Sa'idi, who said: The Amir Abu al-Hasan 'Ali ibn al-Hasan al-Simjuri al-Qa'ini, may Allah have mercy on him, narrated to me on Saturday, the last day of Rajab, may Allah magnify its blessing, in the year 453, saying: I was, the day before yesterday, between sleeping and waking, and I saw as if you had attended me and said to me that the Sufis had made your son Muhammad their deputy in presiding over the session. Just as I heard this statement from you, I saw the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, sitting as if reclining, with his head uncovered, and beside him a person I knew to be 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her. Then your son recited before the Messenger of Allah the composed poem on belief, whose opening is: 'With the praise of Allah I begin the discourse, and His favors are great, exalted is He' from the recitation of the master Imam Abu al-Qasim 'Abd al-Karim ibn Hawazin al-Qushayri. Then something occurred on the tongue of your son Muhammad during his recitation of this poem before the Messenger of Allah, so the Messenger of Allah said to him: 'How did you say?' as if correcting him. So he went back to some verses before it and recited them before the Messenger of Allah along with the verse he had reached, and he recited them as he, peace be upon him, was pleased, until he finished reciting the entire poem. Then the Messenger of Allah said to him: 'I have made you my deputy in presiding over the session.' Then immediately Fatima, peace be upon her, came and sat between the Messenger of Allah and 'A'isha. The Messenger of Allah said to her, 'He will...' meaning regarding what was between her and her daughter after she had risen from his side at the time of this boy's recitation. And I saw upon your son in that state white clothes. Then the Amir Abu al-Hasan al-Simjuri mentioned this vision before a group of Sufis in Nishapur in the khaniqah of Shaykh Abu 'Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami, and all of them were amazed by this glad tiding. The Amir Abu al-Hasan, may Allah have mercy on him, died in the month of Ramadan in the year 454.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a dream narrative transmitted by a chain of authorities, ultimately from the Amir Abu al-Hasan al-Simjuri. It begins as part of a biographical entry for Imam Abu 'Abd Allah al-Farawi. The dreamer (the Amir) describes a state between sleep and wakefulness where he sees a man (the father of Muhammad al-Farawi) appearing to him, informing him that the Sufis have appointed his son Muhammad as their deputy to lead a gathering. Immediately the Prophet appears, sitting with his head uncovered, with 'A'isha beside him. Then the son recites a creed poem by al-Qushayri. When the son hesitates or misrecites, the Prophet corrects him, and the son recites correctly until the end. The Prophet then appoints him as his deputy to lead the session. Then Fatima arrives and sits between the Prophet and 'A'isha. The Prophet speaks to her about something. The son is seen wearing white clothes. The Amir then relates this vision to a group of Sufis, who rejoice at the good news. The passage ends with the death date of the Amir. The narrative serves to validate the orthodoxy of al-Qushayri's creed poem and to elevate the status of Muhammad al-Farawi through the Prophet's direct appointment.

Key Terms

القصيدة المنظومة في الاعتقاد
عقد المجلس
الخانقاه
القشيري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dream is a literal historical event that physically occurred.
Correction: The passage explicitly states it was a vision ('between sleeping and waking'), and it is understood as a spiritual experience, not a material event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Humble Scholar: Muhammad al-Farawi
Biography of Muhammad al-Farawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the character and scholarly path of Muhammad al-Farawi, especially his combination of high learning with humility and self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
A scholar's true greatness lies in humility and self-reliance.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin introduces us to the Imam and faqih of the Haram, Muhammad al-Farawi. Through a biographical sketch, we see a scholar who was deeply learned, yet chose to work as a scribe to maintain his independence. His story illustrates the ideal of the pious scholar who shuns worldly favors and protects his dignity through honest work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow: it begins with a dream, then moves to his education and character.
Notice the author's praise of al-Farawi's humility.
2
Understand the dream episode
Al-Farawi sought permission from the Prophet's garden to hold teaching sessions. This shows his reverence and spiritual orientation.
The narrator says his life itself was proof of virtue.
3
Trace his education
Identify his teachers: Zayn al-Islam and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He studied fiqh, usul, tafsir, and became a prominent student.
He was also raised among Sufis, absorbing their blessings.
4
Reflect on his humility and work
He worked as a scribe of legal documents to avoid depending on favors. This was a deliberate act to preserve his honor and independence.
He married into a family to maintain connection and used earnings for livelihood.
5
Consider his teaching and legacy
He taught at the Nasihiyya school, led prayer at a mosque, and collected many hadith from contemporary scholars. He spread knowledge widely.
He never transgressed the limits of scholars or the righteous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل قال ذكر لي الإمام محمد أنه لما فرغ من زيارة قبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين كان بالمدينة وأراد أن يخرج من المسجد تذكر هذه الرؤيا فوقف واستأذن من الروضة في عقد المجلس كما أشار إليه في الرؤيا فوجد شبه تعريف أنه أذن له فيه والله أعلم قال عبد الغافر وهذا وأمثاله مما يشاهد من أحواله وسيرته عيانا لا يحتاج إلى الاستضاءة فيه بنقل رؤيا أو حكاية وقال عبد الغافر أيضا : محمد بن الفضل بن أحمد أبو عبد الله الصاعدي الفراوي الإمام فقيه الحرم البارع فى الفقه والأصول الحافظ للقواعد نشأ بين الصوفية في حجورهم ووصل إليه بركات أنفاسهم سمع التصانيف والأصول من الإمام زين الإسلام ودرس عليه الأصول والتفسير ثم اختلف إلى مجلس إمام الحرمين ولازم درسه ما عاش وتفقه عليه وعلق عنه الأصول وصار من جملة المذكورين من أصحابه وخرج حاجاً إلى مكة وعقد المجلس ببغداد وسائر البلاد وأظهر العلم بالحرمين وكان منه بهما أثر وذكر ونشر العلم وعاد إلى نيسابور وما تعدى قط حد العلماء ولا سيرة الصالحين من التواضع والتبذل في الملابس والمعايش وتستر بكتبة الشروط لاتصاله بالزمرة الشحامية مصاهرة ليصون بها عرضه وعلمه عن توقع الارفاق ويتبلغ بما يكتسبه منها في أسباب المعيشة من فنون الارزاق وقعد للتدريس في المدرسة الناصحية برأس سكة عمار وإفادة الطلبة فيها وقام بامامة مسجد أبي بكر المطرز وقد سمع المسانيد والصحاح وأكثر عن مشايخ عصره مثل أبي الحسين عبد الغافر وأبي سعد الجنزورى وأبي سعيد الخشاب الصوفي وطبقتهم وله
And the shaykh Abu al-Hasan 'Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il wrote to me, saying: The Imam Muhammad informed me that when he finished visiting the grave of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, when he was in Medina and wanted to leave the mosque, he remembered this vision, so he stopped and asked permission from the [Prophet's] garden to preside over a session as was indicated in the vision, and he found something like an indication that he was granted permission for it, and Allah knows best. 'Abd al-Ghafir said: This and similar things are observed from his states and conduct by eye-witness, not needing illumination by transmission of a vision or story. And 'Abd al-Ghafir also said: Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Ahmad Abu 'Abd Allah al-Sa'idi al-Farawi, the Imam, the faqih of the Haram, proficient in fiqh and usul, preserver of the principles, grew up among the Sufis in their laps and the blessings of their breaths reached him. He heard the books and principles from the Imam Zayn al-Islam and studied with him usul and tafsir, then he attended the circle of Imam al-Haramayn and kept to his lessons as long as he lived, studied fiqh with him, and recorded from him usul, and became among the mentioned ones of his companions. He went out as a pilgrim to Mecca and presided over sessions in Baghdad and other lands, and made knowledge manifest in the two Harams, and there was from him in them effect and mention. He spread knowledge and returned to Nishapur, and he never once transgressed the limit of the scholars nor the way of the righteous in humility and simplicity in clothing and living. He concealed himself by writing legal documents due to his connection with the clan of al-Shihamiyya through marriage, in order to protect his honor and knowledge from expectation of favors, and he would suffice himself with what he earned from that among the means of living from various types of provisions. He sat for teaching in the Nasihiyya school at the head of Sikkat 'Ammar, benefiting students there, and he served as imam of the mosque of Abu Bakr al-Mutarrz. He had heard the musnads and the sahihs and collected much from his contemporary shaykhs such as Abu al-Husayn 'Abd al-Ghafir, Abu Sa'd al-Janziuri, Abu Sa'id al-Khashshab al-Sufi, and their class, and he has [compilations].
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage first describes how Muhammad al-Farawi acted upon the dream: when he visited Medina, he asked permission from the Prophet's grave (rawdah) to preside over a teaching session, and felt he received permission. The narrator, 'Abd al-Ghafir, comments that al-Farawi's life itself testified to his virtue, without needing dreams. Then a full biography is given: his lineage, his epithets as Imam and faqih of the Haram, his expertise in fiqh and usul. He was raised among Sufis, absorbing their blessings. He studied under Zayn al-Islam (likely al-Juwayni? Actually Zayn al-Islam is a title for al-Ghazali? But here it might be someone else) and then under Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He became a distinguished student. He performed Hajj, taught in Baghdad and other cities, and made a mark in Mecca and Medina. He returned to Nishapur and lived humbly, even working as a scribe of legal documents to avoid reliance on patrons, marrying into the Shihami family to protect his dignity. He taught at a school, led prayer at a mosque, and collected hadith from many scholars. The biography emphasizes his piety, humility, and dedication to teaching.

Misconception: Working as a scribe was a demotion or a sign of poverty.
Correction: Al-Farawi deliberately chose to work as a scribe to protect his honor and avoid expecting favors, showing his humility and self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
You read that al-Farawi 'never once transgressed the limit of the scholars nor the way of the righteous' in humility. Think of a recent situation where you felt the urge to display your knowledge or status. How can you lower yourself so that others see only Allah's favor, not your own worth?

Vision of the Prophet Authorizing a Majlis
Ibn Asakir recounts a vision authorizing a scholarly majlis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a prophetic vision could directly authorize a scholarly gathering and its role in establishing spiritual authority in Islamic biography.

Core Idea
A prophetic vision can directly commission a scholar's teaching authority.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari records a remarkable vision in which the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) personally authorizes a person to preside over a scholarly gathering. Such narratives are not merely incidental; they serve to validate the spiritual lineage and authority of scholars in Islamic tradition. Let us examine the account and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice the vivid sequence of events: the Prophet's pleasure, the appointment, the arrival of Fatima, and the white garments.
The Arabic may have missing initial context; focus on the core narrative.
2
Identify key elements of the vision
Identify the main actions: the Prophet appointing a deputy, the presence of Fatima and Aisha, and the mention of compensation. Note the detail of white garments as a symbol of blessing.
3

Ask yourself: Why is this vision recorded? How does it serve to authorize the scholarly gathering? The commentary will help explain that such visions were used to validate spiritual authority.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما رضى عليه السلام إلى أن فرغ من إنشاد تمام القصيدة ثم قال إء رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم جعلتك نائبى فى عقد المجلس ثم في الحال جاءت فاطمة عليها السلام وجلست بين رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عائشة فقال لها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يغرمها ، يعنى على ما وبين فاتها بعد قيامها من جنبه حال إنشاد هذا الصبى ورأيت على ولدك في تلك الحالة ثيابا بيضا ثم ذكر الأمير أبو الحسن السيمجوري هذه الرؤيا بين يدى جماعة المتصوفة بنيسابور في خانقاه الشيخ أبي عبد الرحمن السلمي فكلهم أعجبوا بهذه البشارة ، توفى الأمير أبو الحسن رحمه الله في شهر رمضان سنة ثلاث وخمسين وأربعمائة
He (the Prophet) was pleased until he finished reciting the entire poem, then he said: 'O Messenger of God, I have appointed you as my deputy to preside over the gathering.' Then immediately Fatima (peace be upon her) came and sat between the Messenger of God (may God bless him and grant him peace) and Aisha. The Messenger of God said to her: 'He will compensate her,' meaning for what was between them after she had stood up from his side while this boy was reciting. And I saw upon your child in that state white garments. Then the Amir Abu al-Hasan al-Simjuri related this vision before a group of Sufis in Nishapur in the khaniqah of Shaykh Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami, and they were all amazed by this good news. The Amir Abu al-Hasan died in the month of Ramadan in the year 453 AH.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage narrates a specific vision (ru'ya) of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) that was seen by a person (likely the narrator of the story) in which the Prophet appoints him as his deputy to hold a scholarly gathering (majlis). The sequence includes the Prophet reciting a poem, then Fatima (peace be upon her) arriving and sitting between the Prophet and Aisha, and the Prophet indicating that compensation would be given for something that had occurred. The vision also describes seeing a child (perhaps the subject of the biography) wearing white garments. The Amir Abu al-Hasan al-Simjuri later related this vision to a gathering of Sufis in the khaniqah of Shaykh Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami in Nishapur, and they were all impressed by this good tidings. The passage concludes with the death date of the Amir. The narrative serves to authenticate the spiritual authority and permission for the scholarly gathering, a common theme in hagiographical and biographical works.

Key Terms

المجلس
بشارة
نائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The vision is merely a dream and not a real commissioning.
Correction: In Islamic tradition, visions (ru'ya) of the Prophet are considered real spiritual events, especially when they convey specific commands or authorizations. This vision is presented as a genuine encounter that grants authority to the recipient.

C) 'You will be granted Paradise.'
D) 'Go and teach the people.'

Key Takeaway
Prophetic visions can serve to authenticate and authorize scholarly activities in Islamic tradition.

?

?
The narrator saw a child in white garments during the vision. Purity and light are often symbols of divine favor. In what areas of your life have you settled for spiritual gray instead of striving for the white garments of sincerity and purity?

Seeking Permission from the Prophet’s Grave for a Majlis
Ibn Asakir reports a letter about seeking permission from the Prophet's grave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the practice of seeking spiritual permission from the Prophet’s grave as illustrated by Imam Muhammad’s actions in Medina.

Core Idea
Seeking permission from the Prophet’s grave is a spiritual tradition, not a literal communication.

This lesson presents a moving account from the biography of Imam Muhammad, who after visiting the Prophet’s grave in Medina, sought permission to hold a scholarly gathering there. The story illustrates how the early scholars viewed the Rawdah as a place of spiritual authorization. Through this narrative, we see the tangible connection between the living and the Prophet in traditional Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the sequence: visit, remembrance, seeking permission, indication of permission.
The story is brief but rich in meaning.
2
Understand isti'dhan (seeking permission)
Consider what 'seeking permission' means in this context. It is a spiritual act of respect and baraka, not a literal request to a deceased person.
This is a key concept in traditional practices.
3
Reflect on Abd al-Ghafir's comment
Abd al-Ghafir says such experiences are clear and need no extra validation. This shows trust in spiritual insight and direct witnessing.
4
Consider broader context
This practice is part of tawassul and visiting the grave. Reflect on how it fits into Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل قال ذكر لي الإمام محمد أنه لما فرغ من زيارة قبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين كان بالمدينة وأراد أن يخرج من المسجد تذكر هذه الرؤيا فوقف واستأذن من الروضة في عقد المجلس، كما أشار إليه في الرؤيا فوجد شبه تعريف أنه أذن له فيه والله أعلم ، قال عبد الغافر وهذا وأمثاله مما يشاهد من أحواله وسيرته عيانا لا يحتاج إلى الاستضاءة فيه بنقل رؤيا أو حكاية
And he wrote to Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il, who said: Imam Muhammad mentioned to me that when he finished visiting the grave of the Prophet (may God bless him and grant him peace) while he was in Medina and wanted to leave the mosque, he remembered this vision, so he stopped and sought permission from the Rawdah (the Prophet's tomb area) to hold the gathering, as had been indicated to him in the vision. He found something like an indication that permission had been granted to him in that regard, and God knows best. Abd al-Ghafir said: This and similar instances are witnessed from his states and conduct firsthand, and do not require illumination through the transmission of a vision or a story.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a report from a letter by Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il, transmitting a story from Imam Muhammad. When Imam Muhammad was in Medina and visited the Prophet's grave, he remembered the earlier vision (the one mentioned in the previous lesson) that had authorized him to hold a scholarly gathering. He then stopped and sought permission (ista'dhana) from the Rawdah (the area of the Prophet's tomb) to actually hold the gathering, as the vision had suggested. He perceived a subtle indication that permission was granted. Abd al-Ghafir comments that such spiritual experiences are clear from Imam Muhammad's character and do not require additional verification. This highlights the practice of seeking permission from the Prophet's grave as a form of spiritual authorization, common in Sufi and traditional circles.

Key Terms
الروضة
استأذن
تعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He wrote a book about the vision.

Key Takeaway
Imam Muhammad sought spiritual permission from the Prophet's grave to hold a gathering, demonstrating the baraka and connection with the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The author describes that Imam Muhammad's state was so clear that it needed no external verification. How clear is your own spiritual state to those around you? What would others say about your connection to the Prophet based on your actions?

The Scholarly Lineage of Imam al-Farawi
Ibn Asakir details al-Farawi's teachers and his rise as a scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key teachers who shaped Imam al-Farawi's education, especially Imam Zayn al-Islam and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni.

Core Idea
Al-Farawi's teachers were the giants of his era.

In this passage from the biography of Imam al-Farawi, we learn about his early training. He was raised among Sufis and studied under the leading scholars of Nishapur. His connection to Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni elevated his status as a jurist. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Read the passage that describes al-Farawi's name, upbringing, and early studies.
Notice the titles 'فهقيه الحرم' and 'إمام الحرمين'.
2
Identify the teachers
Find Imam Zayn al-Islam and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and note what subjects he studied with each.
With Zayn al-Islam: usul and tafsir; with Juwayni: fiqh and usul.
3
Understand the significance
Reflect on why studying with Imam al-Haramayn made al-Farawi a distinguished companion.
Being among his 'jullet al-madhkurin' indicates high status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال عبد الغافر أيضا : محمد بن الفضل بن أحمد أبو عبد الله الصاعدي الفراوي الإمام فقيه الحرم البارع فى الفقه والأصول الحافظ للقواعد نشأ بين الصوفية في حجورهم ووصل إليه بركات أنفاسهم ، سمع التصانيف والأصول من الإمام زين الإسلام ودرس عليه الأصول والتفسير ، ثم اختلف إلى مجلس إمام الحرمين ولازم درسه ما عاش وتفقه عليه وعلق عنه الأصول وصار من جملة المذكورين من أصحابه وخرج حاجاً إلى مكة وعقد المجلس ببغداد وسائر البلاد وأظهر العلم بالحرمين وكان منه بهما أثر ،وذكر، ونشر العلم وعاد إلى نيسابور وما تعدى قط حد العلماء ولا سيرة الصالحين من التواضع والتبذل في الملابس والمعايش ، وتستر بكتبة الشروط لاتصاله بالزمرة الشحامية مصاهرة ليصون بها عرضه وعلمه عن توقع الارفاق ويتبلغ بما يكتسبه منها في أسباب المعيشة من فنون الارزاق ، وقعد للتدريس في المدرسة الناصحية برأس سكة عمار وإفادة الطلبة فيها وقام بامامة مسجد أبي بكر المطرز وقد سمع المسانيد والصحاح وأكثر عن مشايخ عصره مثل أبي الحسين عبد الغافر وأبى سعد الجنزورى وأبى سعيد الخشاب الصوفي وطبقتهم وله مجالس الوعظ والتذكير المشحونة بالفوائد والمبالغة في النصح وحكايات المشايخ وذكر أحوالهم. وإلى الإمام محمد الفراوى كانت رحلتى الثانية لأنه كان المقصود بالرحلة في تلك الناحية لما اجتمع فيه من علو الاسناد ووفور العلم ، وصحة الاعتقاد وحسن الخلق ولين الجانب والأقبال بكليته على الطالب ، فاقمت في صحبته سنة كاملة وغنمت من مسموعاته فوائد حسنة طائلة وكان مكرماً لموردى عليه عارفا بحق قصدى إليه ، ومرض مرضة في مدة مقامى عنده نهاه الطبيب عن التمكين من القراءة عليه فيها وعرفه أن ذلك ربما كان سببا لزيادة تألمه ، فقال لا أستجيز أن أمنعهم من القراءة وربما أكون قد حبست في الدنيا لأجلهم ، فكنت اقرأ عليه في حالة مرضه وهو ملقى على فراشه ، ثم عوفى من تلك المرضة وفارقته متوجها إلى هراة فقال لى حين ودعته بعد أن أظهر الجزع لفراقي : ربما لا تلقاني بعد هذا فكان كما قال فجاءنا نعيه إلى هراة وكان موته فى العشر من شوال سنة ثلاثين وخمساية ودفن في تربة أبي بكر ابن خزيمة
And Abd al-Ghafir also said: Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Ahmad Abu Abd Allah al-Sa'idi al-Farawi, the Imam, the jurist of the Haram, excellent in fiqh and usul, memorizer of the principles, grew up among the Sufis in their laps and received the blessings of their breaths. He heard the books and the principles from Imam Zayn al-Islam, and studied with him usul and tafsir. Then he frequented the circle of Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) and attended his lessons as long as he lived, studied fiqh with him, and wrote down from him usul, becoming one of his distinguished companions. He traveled to Mecca for pilgrimage, held scholarly gatherings in Baghdad and other lands, manifesting knowledge in the Haramayn, and left a positive impact there. He spread knowledge and returned to Nishapur, never exceeding the bounds of scholars or the conduct of the righteous in humility and simplicity in clothing and livelihood. He concealed himself by writing legal documents due to his connection with the Shahamiyya family through marriage, in order to protect his honor and knowledge from expectation of favors, and he would live on what he earned from that in various ways of sustenance. He sat for teaching at the Nasihiyya school at the head of Sikka Ammar, benefitting students there, and led the prayer at the mosque of Abu Bakr al-Mutarriz. He heard the Musanid and Sahih collections extensively from the shaykhs of his time, such as Abu al-Husayn Abd al-Ghafir, Abu Sa'd al-Janzuri, Abu Sa'id al-Khashshab al-Sufi, and their peers. He had assemblies of exhortation and remembrance filled with benefits, earnestness in advice, stories of the shaykhs, and mention of their states. To Imam Muhammad al-Farawi was my second journey, because he was the goal of travel in that region due to his high chain of transmission, abundant knowledge, sound belief, good character, gentleness, and complete attention to the student. So I stayed with him for a full year and gained from what I heard from him great and abundant benefits. He was generous to those who visited him, recognizing the right of my intention to him. He fell ill during my stay with him. The doctor forbade him from allowing reading to him (students reciting) during that illness, informing him that it might increase his pain. He said: 'I do not deem it permissible to prevent them from reading; perhaps I have been detained in this world for their sake.' So I would read to him during his illness while he lay on his bed. Then he recovered from that illness, and I left him heading to Herat. He said to me when I bid him farewell, after showing distress at my departure: 'Perhaps you will not meet me after this.' And so it happened as he said: news of his death came to us in Herat. His death was in the last ten days of Shawwal in the year 530 AH, and he was buried in the cemetery of Abu Bakr ibn Khuzayma.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biography of Imam Muhammad al-Farawi, written by Abd al-Ghafir (or Ibn Asakir quoting him). It highlights his scholarly excellence, his study with Zayn al-Islam and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), his travels, and his teaching. He was known for his humility, living simply, and working as a legal document writer to avoid dependence on gifts. Despite his status, he taught at a school and led prayers. The biographer (Ibn Asakir) mentions that he personally traveled to study with al-Farawi, describing his qualities as a teacher, including his willingness to continue teaching even when ill. The story of his illness and death shows his dedication: when the doctor forbade students from reading to him, al-Farawi insisted on allowing them, thinking that perhaps he was kept alive for their benefit. His prediction of not meeting again came true. The biography concludes with his death date and burial place.

Key Terms
فقيه الحرم
إمام الحرمين
زين الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Zayn al-Islam
C) Abd al-Ghafir
D) Abu Bakr al-Mutarriz

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Farawi's scholarly foundation came from studying under Zayn al-Islam and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni.

?
The passage says al-Farawi 'frequented the circle of Imam al-Haramayn and attended his lessons as long as he lived.' When was the last time you committed to a teacher or a lesson with such persistence? What is stopping you from being that consistent today?

Humility and Livelihood of Imam al-Farawi
Ibn Asakir describes al-Farawi's humility, work, and teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Farawi balanced scholarship with humility and earning a living to preserve his integrity.

Core Idea
Al-Farawi worked as a legal writer to avoid relying on gifts.

Imam al-Farawi's biography reveals his remarkable humility and practical wisdom. Despite his high scholarly status, he took up work as a legal document writer to support himself without seeking favors. This passage illustrates his simple lifestyle and teaching efforts.

He fell ill during my stay with him. The doctor forbade him from allowing reading to him (students reciting) during that illness, informing him that it might increase his pain. He said: 'I do not deem it permissible to prevent them from reading; perhaps I have been detained in this world for their sake.' So I would read to him during his illness while he lay on his bed. Then he recovered from that illness, and I left him heading to Herat. He said to me when I bid him farewell, after showing distress at my departure: 'Perhaps you will not meet me after this.' And so it happened as he said: news of his death came to us in Herat. His death was in the last ten days of Shawwal in the year 530 AH, and he was buried in the cemetery of Abu Bakr ibn Khuzayma.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biography of Imam Muhammad al-Farawi, written by Abd al-Ghafir (or Ibn Asakir quoting him). It highlights his scholarly excellence, his study with Zayn al-Islam and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), his travels, and his teaching. He was known for his humility, living simply, and working as a legal document writer to avoid dependence on gifts. Despite his status, he taught at a school and led prayers. The biographer (Ibn Asakir) mentions that he personally traveled to study with al-Farawi, describing his qualities as a teacher, including his willingness to continue teaching even when ill. The story of his illness and death shows his dedication: when the doctor forbade students from reading to him, al-Farawi insisted on allowing them, thinking that perhaps he was kept alive for their benefit. His prediction of not meeting again came true. The biography concludes with his death date and burial place.

Key Terms
المدرسة الناصحية
كتبة الشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Working as a legal document writer is beneath a scholar's dignity.
Correction: Al-Farawi did so to preserve his honor and avoid depending on gifts, showing piety and self-sufficiency.

Checkpoint

C) Trade
D) Farming

Key Takeaway

?

?
Al-Farawi concealed his knowledge by working with his hands. Do you ever use your knowledge or status to seek recognition or favors? How can you practice true humility in your daily interactions starting tomorrow?

The Dedication of Imam al-Farawi to His Students
Ibn Asakir recounts his own study with al-Farawi and the imam's dedication during illness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the deep dedication of Imam al-Farawi to his students, as exemplified by his insistence on teaching despite illness.

Core Idea
Al-Farawi continued teaching even when ill, believing he was kept alive for his students.

In the most moving part of the biography, Ibn Asakir recounts his personal experience with Imam al-Farawi. The imam's devotion to his students shines through when, despite a doctor's advice, he refuses to stop teaching. This lesson explores that story and its lesson on priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the personal account
Read from 'وإلى الإمام محمد الفراوى' to the end, noting that this is from Ibn Asakir's own experience.
This is a first-person narrative.
2
Identify why he was the goal of travel
Find the qualities that made al-Farawi the destination: 'علو الاسناد' etc.
Also note 'الأقبال بكليته على الطالب'.
3

Read the doctor's advice and al-Farawi's response. Consider his selflessness.
He said: 'لا أستجيز أن أمنعهم من القراءة وربما أكون قد حبست في الدنيا لأجلهم'.
4

See how he predicted that they would not meet again, and how it came true.
He died in Shawwal 530 AH.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال عبد الغافر أيضا : محمد بن الفضل بن أحمد أبو عبد الله الصاعدي الفراوي الإمام فقيه الحرم البارع فى الفقه والأصول الحافظ للقواعد نشأ بين الصوفية في حجورهم ووصل إليه بركات أنفاسهم ، سمع التصانيف والأصول من الإمام زين الإسلام ودرس عليه الأصول والتفسير ، ثم اختلف إلى مجلس إمام الحرمين ولازم درسه ما عاش وتفقه عليه وعلق عنه الأصول وصار من جملة المذكورين من أصحابه وخرج حاجاً إلى مكة وعقد المجلس ببغداد وسائر البلاد وأظهر العلم بالحرمين وكان منه بهما أثر ،وذكر، ونشر العلم وعاد إلى نيسابور وما تعدى قط حد العلماء ولا سيرة الصالحين من التواضع والتبذل في الملابس والمعايش ، وتستر بكتبة الشروط لاتصاله بالزمرة الشحامية مصاهرة ليصون بها عرضه وعلمه عن توقع الارفاق ويتبلغ بما يكتسبه منها في أسباب المعيشة من فنون الارزاق ، وقعد للتدريس في المدرسة الناصحية برأس سكة عمار وإفادة الطلبة فيها وقام بامامة مسجد أبي بكر المطرز وقد سمع المسانيد والصحاح وأكثر عن مشايخ عصره مثل أبي الحسين عبد الغافر وأبى سعد الجنزورى وأبى سعيد الخشاب الصوفي وطبقتهم وله مجالس الوعظ والتذكير المشحونة بالفوائد والمبالغة في النصح وحكايات المشايخ وذكر أحوالهم. وإلى الإمام محمد الفراوى كانت رحلتى الثانية لأنه كان المقصود بالرحلة في تلك الناحية لما اجتمع فيه من علو الاسناد ووفور العلم ، وصحة الاعتقاد وحسن الخلق ولين الجانب والأقبال بكليته على الطالب ، فاقمت في صحبته سنة كاملة وغنمت من مسموعاته فوائد حسنة طائلة وكان مكرماً لموردى عليه عارفا بحق قصدى إليه ، ومرض مرضة في مدة مقامى عنده نهاه الطبيب عن التمكين من القراءة عليه فيها وعرفه أن ذلك ربما كان سببا لزيادة تألمه ، فقال لا أستجيز أن أمنعهم من القراءة وربما أكون قد حبست في الدنيا لأجلهم ، فكنت اقرأ عليه في حالة مرضه وهو ملقى على فراشه ، ثم عوفى من تلك المرضة وفارقته متوجها إلى هراة فقال لى حين ودعته بعد أن أظهر الجزع لفراقي : ربما لا تلقاني بعد هذا فكان كما قال فجاءنا نعيه إلى هراة وكان موته فى العشر من شوال سنة ثلاثين وخمساية ودفن في تربة أبي بكر ابن خزيمة
And Abd al-Ghafir also said: Muhammad ibn al-Fadl ibn Ahmad Abu Abd Allah al-Sa'idi al-Farawi, the Imam, the jurist of the Haram, excellent in fiqh and usul, memorizer of the principles, grew up among the Sufis in their laps and received the blessings of their breaths. He heard the books and the principles from Imam Zayn al-Islam, and studied with him usul and tafsir. Then he frequented the circle of Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) and attended his lessons as long as he lived, studied fiqh with him, and wrote down from him usul, becoming one of his distinguished companions. He traveled to Mecca for pilgrimage, held scholarly gatherings in Baghdad and other lands, manifesting knowledge in the Haramayn, and left a positive impact there. He spread knowledge and returned to Nishapur, never exceeding the bounds of scholars or the conduct of the righteous in humility and simplicity in clothing and livelihood. He concealed himself by writing legal documents due to his connection with the Shahamiyya family through marriage, in order to protect his honor and knowledge from expectation of favors, and he would live on what he earned from that in various ways of sustenance. He sat for teaching at the Nasihiyya school at the head of Sikka Ammar, benefitting students there, and led the prayer at the mosque of Abu Bakr al-Mutarriz. He heard the Musanid and Sahih collections extensively from the shaykhs of his time, such as Abu al-Husayn Abd al-Ghafir, Abu Sa'd al-Janzuri, Abu Sa'id al-Khashshab al-Sufi, and their peers. He had assemblies of exhortation and remembrance filled with benefits, earnestness in advice, stories of the shaykhs, and mention of their states. To Imam Muhammad al-Farawi was my second journey, because he was the goal of travel in that region due to his high chain of transmission, abundant knowledge, sound belief, good character, gentleness, and complete attention to the student. So I stayed with him for a full year and gained from what I heard from him great and abundant benefits. He was generous to those who visited him, recognizing the right of my intention to him. He fell ill during my stay with him. The doctor forbade him from allowing reading to him (students reciting) during that illness, informing him that it might increase his pain. He said: 'I do not deem it permissible to prevent them from reading; perhaps I have been detained in this world for their sake.' So I would read to him during his illness while he lay on his bed. Then he recovered from that illness, and I left him heading to Herat. He said to me when I bid him farewell, after showing distress at my departure: 'Perhaps you will not meet me after this.' And so it happened as he said: news of his death came to us in Herat. His death was in the last ten days of Shawwal in the year 530 AH, and he was buried in the cemetery of Abu Bakr ibn Khuzayma.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a detailed biography of Imam Muhammad al-Farawi, written by Abd al-Ghafir (or Ibn Asakir quoting him). It highlights his scholarly excellence, his study with Zayn al-Islam and Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), his travels, and his teaching. He was known for his humility, living simply, and working as a legal document writer to avoid dependence on gifts. Despite his status, he taught at a school and led prayers. The story of his illness and death shows his dedication: when the doctor forbade students from reading to him, al-Farawi insisted on allowing them, thinking that perhaps he was kept alive for their benefit. His prediction of not meeting again came true. The biography concludes with his death date and burial place.

Correction: Al-Farawi believed that his continued existence was for the benefit of his students, so he allowed them to read to him despite the doctor's orders.

Biography of Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani and the Method of Reading al-Irshad
Ibn Asakir provides a biography of Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani with an anecdote about reading al-Irshad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of studying kalam texts for understanding (diraya) rather than mere transmission (riwaya), as exemplified by Imam al-Kirmani's correction of a student.

Core Idea
Knowledge of kalam requires deep understanding, not just narration.

This passage from Ibn Asakir presents the biography of Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani, a prominent scholar of usul and fiqh. It includes a valuable anecdote where he instructs a student on the proper method of reading al-Juwayni's al-Irshad, highlighting the distinction between learning for transmission and learning for comprehension.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography
Begin by reading the passage to learn about al-Kirmani's birth, teachers, status, and his meeting with Ibn Asakir. Note his connection to al-Qushayri and al-Juwayni.
2
Identify the anecdote
Focus on the interaction with the Baghdadi student. Notice how the student begins reading al-Irshad in the manner of hadith transmission, and al-Kirmani's corrective response.
Al-Kirmani distinguishes between riwaya (transmission) and diraya (understanding).
3
Reflect on the pedagogical lesson
Consider why al-Kirmani insisted on reading 'little by little for understanding.' What does this teach about the nature of kalam as a discipline?
This connects to the learning objective about the methods of studying theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم شيخنا الإمام أبو سعد إسماعيل بن أبي صالح أحمد بن عبد الملك بن على بن عبد الصمد النيسابوري المعروف بالكرماني سئل عن مولده وأنا أسمع فقال في أوائل ذي القعدة سنة اثنتين وخمسين وأربعمائة ، تفقه على الاستاذ أبى القسم القشيري والإمام أبي المعالي الجويني وكان إماماً في الأصول والفقه ، حسن النظر مقدماً في التذكير ، سمع الحديث الكثير بافادة والده أبي صالح الحافظ المعروف بالمؤذن ، وخرج له والده الفوائد وسكن كرمان إلى أن مات بها وكان وجيهاً عند سلطانها معظماً في أهلها ، محترماً بين العلماء في سائر البلاد، لقيته ببغداد سنة إحدى وعشرين وخمسماية وسمعت منه وسأله بعض البغداديين هل قرأت كتاب الارشاد على الإمام أبى المعالى ، فقال نعم ، فاستأذنه في قراءته عليه ، فأذن له ، فشرع في قراءته على عادة أصحاب الحديث فلما قرأ منه نحو صفحة قال له إن هذا العلم لا يقرأ كما يقرأ الحديث للرواية وإنما يقرأ شيئاً شيئاً للدراية ، فإن أردت أن تقرأه كما قرأناه
Among them is our shaykh, the Imam Abu Sa'd Isma'il ibn Abi Salih Ahmad ibn Abd al-Malik ibn Ali ibn Abd al-Samad al-Nisaburi, known as al-Kirmani. I (Ibn Asakir) heard him being asked about his birth, and he said: 'In the beginning of Dhu al-Qa'dah of the year 452 AH.' He studied fiqh under the master Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri and Imam Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni, and he was an imam in usul and fiqh, with sound reasoning, and prominent in sermonizing. He heard many hadith through the assistance of his father, Abu Salih the hafiz known as al-Mu'adhdhin. His father extracted benefits for him. He settled in Kirman and lived there until he died. He was influential with the ruler, honored among its people, and respected among scholars in all lands. I (Ibn Asakir) met him in Baghdad in the year 521 AH and heard from him. One of the Baghdadis asked him: 'Did you read the book al-Irshad under Imam Abu al-Ma'ali?' He said 'Yes.' So the man asked permission to read it to him, and he allowed it. He began to read it following the custom of hadith scholars. When he had read about a page, he said to him: 'This knowledge is not read as hadith is read for transmission; rather it is read little by little for understanding. If you want to read it as we read it...'
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Abu Sa'd Isma'il al-Kirmani, a scholar from Nishapur who settled in Kirman. It mentions his teachers: Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri and Imam Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni (the same Juwayni known as Imam al-Haramayn). He was an expert in usul and fiqh. He heard much hadith from his father. He was respected by rulers and scholars. Ibn Asakir met him in Baghdad and heard from him. The anecdote illustrates al-Kirmani's approach to teaching: when a student began reading al-Irshad (a kalam work by al-Juwayni) in the manner of hadith transmission (i.e., simply reciting for the sake of hearing and narrating), al-Kirmani corrected him, explaining that such knowledge requires studying piece by piece for understanding (diraya), not just riwaya. The passage ends with his instruction, emphasizing the distinction between transmission and comprehension, a key pedagogical point in Islamic scholarship.

Key Terms
الارشاد
الدراية
الرواية
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that reading a book aloud for transmission is sufficient for learning its content.
Correction: Al-Kirmani explains that for kalam, one must study piece by piece for understanding, not just recite for narration.

Checkpoint
What did al-Kirmani tell the student who began reading al-Irshad like hadith?
A) 'You are reading too fast.'
B) 'This knowledge is read little by little for understanding, not like hadith for transmission.'
C) 'You must memorize the entire book first.'
D) 'This book is not suitable for beginners.'

Key Takeaway
Studying kalam requires active comprehension, not passive recitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a book of Islamic knowledge, do you read it for understanding or just to say you have read it? How can you change your approach to ensure deep comprehension?
?
Al-Kirmani corrected the student’s method. Is there any area of your Islamic learning where you rely on mere reading or memorization instead of deep understanding? What step will you take today to change that?
?
The student began reading al-Irshad as if it were a hadith collection. Are you studying kalam or aqeedah with a teacher who explains the meaning, or are you just reading for credit? What will you do to seek a teacher who teaches for understanding?

The Dedication of Imam al-Farawi
Ibn Asakir recounts his study under al-Farawi and his death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the character and dedication of Imam al-Farawi as depicted by Ibn Asakir, and how scholars prioritized teaching despite personal hardship.

Core Idea
True scholars teach even when death approaches.

This passage gives us a window into the life of Imam Muhammad al-Farawi through the eyes of his student, the great historian Ibn Asakir. It reveals not only the scholar's credentials but his selfless dedication to knowledge. As you read, notice how al-Farawi's actions embody the Islamic ideal of teaching as a means of drawing closer to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجالس الوعظ والتذكير المشحونة بالفوائد والمبالغة في النصح وحكايات المشايخ وذكر أحوالهم. وإلى الإمام محمد الفراوى كانت رحلتي الثانية لأنه كان المقصود بالرحلة في تلك الناحية لما اجتمع فيه من علو الإسناد ووفور العلم، وصحة الاعتقاد وحسن الخلق ولين الجانب والإقبال بكليته على الطالب، فأقمت في صحبته سنة كاملة وغنمت من مسموعاته فوائد حسنة طائلة وكان مكرما لموردي عليه عارفا بحق قصدي إليه، ومرض مرضة في مدة مقامي عنده نهاه الطبيب عن التمكين من القراءة عليه فيها وعرفه أن ذلك ربما كان سببا لزيادة تألمه، فقال لا أستجيز أن أمنعهم من القراءة وربما أكون قد حبست في الدنيا لأجلهم، فكنت أقرأ عليه في حالة مرضه وهو ملقى على فراشه، ثم عوفي من تلك المرضة وفارقته متوجها إلى هراة فقال لي حين ودعته بعد أن أظهر الجزع لفراقي: ربما لا تلقاني بعد هذا فكان كما قال فجاءنا نعيه إلى هراة وكان موته في العشر من شوال سنة ثلاثين وخمسائة ودفن في تربة أبي بكر ابن خزيمة (١). (١) قال الشيخ الإمام أبو جعفر وجدت بخط تاج الدين البندهى وكان موت أبي الله الفراوى رحمه الله في السادس من شوال سنة ثلاثين وخمسائة وهو ابن مائة سنة وخمسة أشهر ودفن في تربة ابن خزيمة (كذا في هامش الأصل).
Sessions of exhortation and reminding filled with benefits and exaggeration in advice and stories of the shaykhs and mention of their conditions. And to Imam Muhammad al-Farawi was my second journey because he was the intended destination for travel in that region due to what was combined in him of high chain of transmission and abundance of knowledge, correctness of belief, good character, softness of side, and complete devotion to the student. So I stayed in his company a complete year and gained from his auditions excellent and immense benefits, and he was generous to whoever came to him, knowing the right of my intent towards him. He fell ill during my stay with him, and the doctor forbade him from allowing reading upon him during it and informed him that that might be a cause for increase of his pain. So he said: 'I do not deem it permissible to prevent them from reading; perhaps I have been detained in the world only for their sake.' So I used to read upon him in his state of illness while he was lying on his bed. Then he was cured from that illness, and I left him heading to Herat. He said to me when I bade him farewell after he showed distress at my departure: 'Perhaps you will not meet me after this.' And it was as he said, for news of his death came to us in Herat. His death was in the first ten days of Shawwal of the year 530, and he was buried in the tomb of Abu Bakr ibn Khuzayma. (1) (1) Shaykh Imam Abu Ja'far said: I found in the handwriting of Taj al-Din al-Bandahi: The death of Abu Allah al-Farawi, may God have mercy on him, was on the sixth of Shawwal of the year 530, and he was one hundred years and five months old, and he was buried in the tomb of Ibn Khuzayma (as in the margin of the original).
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's work. He begins by describing the content of his sessions with his shaykh, then explains why he travelled to al-Farawi: the latter was renowned for high chains, abundant knowledge, correct belief, good character, and dedication to students. Ibn Asakir stayed with him a year, gaining great benefits. He then narrates an episode during al-Farawi's illness: the doctor forbade him from allowing students to read to him, but al-Farawi refused, saying he might have been created only for their sake. Ibn Asakir read to him while he lay ill. After recovery, Ibn Asakir left for Herat, and al-Farawi predicted they would not meet again, which proved true. The death date is given as early Shawwal 530 AH. A marginal note by Shaykh Abu Ja'far cites Taj al-Din al-Bandahi giving an alternative date of 6 Shawwal and stating al-Farawi's age as 100 years and 5 months.

Key Terms
علو الإسناد
صحة الاعتقاد
النعي
تربة ابن خزيمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He said he would only teach a few students.

D) He asked the students to pray for his recovery.

Key Takeaway
Al-Farawi's example shows that true scholars prioritize the transmission of knowledge over personal comfort, even in illness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What would you have done if you were in al-Farawi’s place, lying ill and told by the doctor to stop teaching? Would you prioritize your comfort or your students’ need for knowledge? What does your honest answer reveal about your intentions?
?
Consider the phrase 'perhaps I have been detained in the world only for their sake.' Is there any person or cause for which you feel you are 'detained' in this world? If not, what would need to change in your life for you to have that sense of purpose?
?
Al-Farawi wept at parting from his student, knowing he might not see him again. When you part from someone you have benefited or been benefited by, do you feel that depth of connection? How can you invest more fully in your relationships with teachers or students so that partings are filled with such meaning?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

The Life and Method of Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani
Ibn Asakir relates the biography of Shaykh Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key biographical details of Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani and the methodological distinction he made between studying kalam for understanding (daraya) and transmitting hadith for narration (riwaya).

Core Idea
Kalam must be studied for understanding, not just transmitted for narration.

This lesson introduces the biography of a prominent Shafi'i-Ash'ari scholar, Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani, as recorded by Ibn Asakir. It highlights his scholarly pedigree and a telling anecdote that illustrates a core methodological principle: the study of kalam requires active understanding, not passive transmission. This distinction remains relevant for students of Islamic theology today.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical data
Read the passage and note the key facts: birth date, teachers, expertise, settlement, and death. This gives context to al-Kirmani's authority.
2
Analyze the anecdote
Pay attention to the exchange with the Baghdadi. What did al-Kirmani say about how to read al-Irshad?
The distinction between riwaya (narration) and daraya (understanding) is crucial.
3
Connect to methodology
Think about why al-Kirmani insisted on reading 'part by part for understanding'. What does this teach us about the study of kalam?
Kalam requires active engagement, not just memorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم شيخنا الإمام أبو سعد إسماعيل بن أبي صالح أحمد بن عبد الملك بن علي بن عبد الصمد النيسابوري المعروف بالكرماني سئل عن مولده وأنا أسمع فقال في أوائل ذي القعدة سنة اثنتين وخمسين وأربعمائة، تفقه على الأستاذ أبي القاسم القشيري والإمام أبي المعالي الجويني وكان إماما في الأصول والفقه، حسن النظر مقدما في التذكير، سمع الحديث الكثير بإفادة والده أبي صالح الحافظ المعروف بالمؤذن، وخرج له والده الفوائد وسكن كرمان إلى أن مات بها وكان وجيها عند سلطانها معظما في أهلها، محترما بين العلماء في سائر البلاد، لقيته ببغداد سنة إحدى وعشرين وخمسمائة وسمعت منه وسأله بعض البغداديين هل قرأت كتاب الإرشاد على الإمام أبي المعالي، فقال نعم، فاستأذنه في قراءته عليه، فأذن له، فشرع في قراءته على عادة أصحاب الحديث فلما قرأ منه نحو صفحة قال له إن هذا العلم لا يقرأ كما يقرأ الحديث للرواية وإنما يقرأ شيئا شيئا للدراية، فإن أردت أن تقرأه كما قرأناه وإلا فاتركه، مات سنة إحدى وثلاثين وخمسمائة بكرمان وبلغني وفاته وأنا بأصبهان
And among them is our shaykh, the Imam Abu Sa'd Isma'il ibn Abi Salih Ahmad ibn Abd al-Malik ibn Ali ibn Abd al-Samad al-Naysaburi, known as al-Kirmani. He was asked about his birth while I was listening, and he said: 'In the beginning of Dhu al-Qa'dah of the year 452.' He studied fiqh under al-Ustadh Abu al-Qasim al-Qushayri and Imam Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni, and he was an imam in usul and fiqh, excellent in reasoning, advanced in reminding. He heard much hadith with the aid of his father Abu Salih the hafiz known as al-Mu'adhdhin, and his father compiled for him the benefits. He settled in Kirman and died there. He was esteemed by its ruler, honored among its people, respected among scholars in all lands. I met him in Baghdad in the year 521 and heard from him. One of the Baghdadis asked him: 'Did you read the book al-Irshad to Imam Abu al-Ma'ali?' He said: 'Yes.' So he asked his permission to read it to him, and he permitted him. So he began reading it according to the habit of the hadith scholars. When he had read about a page from it, he said to him: 'This knowledge is not read as hadith is read for narration; rather it is read part by part for understanding. If you wish to read it as we read it, then do so; otherwise leave it.' He died in the year 531 in Kirman, and news of his death reached me while I was in Isfahan.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Abu Sa'd al-Kirmani. Ibn Asakir records his birth date (early Dhu al-Qa'dah 452 AH), his teachers (al-Qushayri and al-Juwayni), his expertise in usul and fiqh, and his hadith learning from his father. He settled in Kirman and was highly respected. Ibn Asakir met him in Baghdad in 521 AH. The passage includes a telling anecdote: a Baghdadi asked to read al-Juwayni's K. al-Irshad to al-Kirmani, who permitted it. But when the man began reading in the manner of hadith recitation (page by page for narration), al-Kirmani stopped him and explained that kalam must be read part by part for understanding (daraya), not for mere narration. This highlights a methodological distinction between teaching kalam and teaching hadith. Al-Kirmani died in 531 AH while Ibn Asakir was in Isfahan.

Key Terms
تفقه
الأصول
التذكير
الدراية
الرواية
الإرشاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Kirmani rejected hadith methodology entirely.
Correction: He distinguished between proper methods for kalam (understanding) and hadith (narration), but did not reject hadith studies.

Checkpoint
What did al-Kirmani tell the Baghdadi who began reading al-Irshad in the style of hadith recitation?
A) He praised his method.
B) He said that kalam must be read for understanding, part by part, not like hadith for narration.
C) He asked him to continue but faster.
D) He refused to teach him altogether.

Key Takeaway
Al-Kirmani taught that kalam, unlike hadith, must be studied part by part for deep understanding, not merely narrated for transmission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study 'aqeedah, do you tend to race through pages to cover material, or do you pause to truly understand each part? Al-Kirmani stopped a student for just reading a page without grasping it. What would he say about your current study habits? Will you commit to reading 'part by part for understanding' starting today?
?
The Baghdadi came with the habit of hadith narration, but al-Kirmani insisted on a deeper engagement. Where in your own life have you been satisfied with simply 'narrating' Islamic knowledge (quoting verses or sayings) without seeking to understand their meaning and implications for your heart and actions? What specific step will you take this week to move from riwaya to diraya in your learning?

The Biography of Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami al-Dimashqi
Ibn Asakir details the scholarly life and death of al-Sulami.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the scholarly stature and blessed life of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami al-Dimashqi as recorded by his student Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
Al-Sulami's life exemplifies devotion to learning and dying in prostration.

Ibn Asakir presents a brief but rich biography of his teacher al-Sulami, highlighting his lineage, teachers, diverse knowledge, and the honor of his death. This lesson introduces you to a scholar who combined deep learning with humility and practical service to the community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the narrative flow.
Notice the chain of teachers mentioned.
2
Identify key teachers
List the scholars who taught al-Sulami, especially Qadi al-Marwazi, Abu al-Fath al-Maqdisi, and Imam al-Ghazali. Note how al-Ghazali appointed him to teach after al-Maqdisi's death.
Al-Ghazali praised his understanding and ordered him to take the lead.
3
Note the sciences he mastered
Al-Sulami was knowledgeable in many fields: tafsir, usul, fiqh, preaching, inheritance laws, arithmetic, dream interpretation, etc. This shows his breadth of knowledge.
His expertise in inheritance and dream interpretation reflects practical and spiritual dimensions.
4
Reflect on his personal qualities
Ibn Asakir describes him as humble, selfless, devoted to spreading knowledge, and gentle. Consider how these traits complement his intellectual achievements.
Humility and sincerity are emphasized.
5
Understand the significance of his death
He died while prostrating in Fajr prayer. This is seen as a blessed end, indicating divine acceptance. Reflect on how his life and death are connected.
This is a key point for inspiration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم شيخنا الإمام أبو الحسن السلمي الدمشقي رحمه الله وهو أبو الحسن علي بن المسلم بن محمد بن علي بن الفتح بن علي السلمي ابن ابنة أبي بكر محمد بن عقيل الشهرزوري، ولد سنة خمسين وأربعمائة أو سنة اثنتين وخمسين وتفقه أولا بالقاضي أبي المظفر عبد الجليل بن عبد الجبار المروزي نزيل دمشق وغيره، وعنى بنفسه بكثرة المطالعة والتكرار ولما قدم الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم المقدسي لازمه وكان معيدا لدرسه ولزم الإمام أبا حامد الغزالي مدة مقامه بدمشق، وهو الذي أمره بالتصدر بعد موت الفقيه نصر وكان يثني على علمه ويصف حسن فهمه، وانتهى إليه أمر التدريس والفتيا والتذكير بدمشق، فكان أجرى أهل زمانه قلما بالفتوى وأغزرهم علما مع التواضع، وقلة الدعوى عالما بالتفسير والأصول والفقه والتذكير والفرائض والحساب والمناسخات والتعبير والمنامات، مع ما رزق من لين الجانب وسلامة الصدر وقضاء حقوق الناس والتوفر على نشر العلم والإرشاد إلى الحق، وتحري الصدق إلى أن قبضه الله إلى رحمته ساجدا في الركعة الثانية من صلاة الصبح يوم الأربعاء الثالث عشر من ذي القعدة سنة ثلاث وثلاثين وخمسمائة.
And among them is our shaykh, the Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami al-Dimashqi, may God have mercy on him. He is Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Fath ibn Ali al-Sulami, the grandson (son of the daughter) of Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Aqil al-Shahrazuri. He was born in the year 450 or 452. He first studied fiqh under Qadi Abu al-Muzaffar Abd al-Jalil ibn Abd al-Jabbar al-Marwazi, the resident of Damascus, and others. He devoted himself to much reading and repetition. When the faqih Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim al-Maqdisi arrived, he accompanied him and was a repeater of his lessons. He adhered to Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali during his stay in Damascus, and it was he who ordered him to take the lead after the death of al-Faqih Nasr, and he used to praise his knowledge and describe his good understanding. The matters of teaching, giving fatwa, and reminding in Damascus came to him. He was the most fluent of his time in writing fatwas and the most abundant in knowledge, along with humility and lack of pretension. He was knowledgeable in tafsir, usul, fiqh, reminding, inheritance law, arithmetic, inheritance distributions, interpretation of dreams, along with what he was given of softness of side, soundness of heart, fulfilling people's rights, devotion to spreading knowledge, guiding to truth, and pursuing honesty until God took him to His mercy while he was prostrating in the second rak'a of the morning prayer on Wednesday, the 13th of Dhu al-Qa'da, year 533.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami al-Dimashqi. Ibn Asakir gives his full lineage, birth around 450-452 AH, early studies with Qadi al-Marwazi, and association with Abu al-Fath al-Maqdisi and Imam al-Ghazali. Al-Ghazali appointed him to teach after al-Maqdisi's death and praised his understanding. Al-Sulami became the leading teacher, mufti, and preacher in Damascus, known for his prolific fatwas, vast knowledge, humility, and sincerity. He excelled in many disciplines: tafsir, usul, fiqh, preaching, inheritance, arithmetic, estate calculations, and dream interpretation. He was gentle, sincere, and devoted to serving people and spreading truth. His death was notable: he died while prostrating in the second rak'a of Fajr prayer on Wednesday, 13 Dhu al-Qa'da 533 AH, indicating a blessed end.

الفتيا
التذكير
الفرائض
المناسخات
التعبير
المنامات

Checkpoint

A) In battle.
B) While sleeping.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sulami died while prostrating in Fajr prayer, a sign of his righteous life and divine favor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that al-Sulami died while prostrating in the second rak‘ah of Fajr. If you were to die in your next prayer, would you be found in a state of humility and devotion? What single habit can you change today to make your prayer more like his?
?
Al-Sulami's knowledge was vast, but the passage emphasizes his humility and lack of pretension. Where in your life do you show off what you know? How can you seek knowledge solely for Allah's sake starting this week?
?
He spent his life teaching, giving fatwas, and guiding others. When was the last time you sacrificed your time to help someone learn about their faith without expecting recognition? Will you commit to one act of teaching or guiding this week?

Biography of Abu Mansur al-Asbahani
Ibn Asakir describes his meeting with al-Asbahani and his death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and scholarly status of Abu Mansur al-Asbahani as described by Ibn Asakir, including his travels, honor by caliph and scholars, and his sudden death.

Core Idea
A scholar's journey and death are recorded as lessons for posterity.

Biographies form a vital part of Islamic learning, preserving the lives of scholars who carried knowledge across lands. In this passage, Ibn Asakir recounts his personal encounters with the eminent scholar Abu Mansur al-Asbahani, highlighting his reception in Baghdad, his teaching sessions in Isfahan, and his sudden death. As you read, note the details that show the respect he commanded and the continuity of scholarly tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a feel for Ibn Asakir's personal narrative style.
Notice how Ibn Asakir uses first-person observations.
2
Identify key events and dates
Find the two meetings in Baghdad (524 AH) and Isfahan (532 AH), and the date of death (536 AH). Note the locations and the caliph's involvement.
These dates help place al-Asbahani in the broader historical context.
3
Reflect on the scholar's qualities
Ibn Asakir describes him as faqih, preacher, mufassir, and mentions his dignity and high rank. Consider what these titles tell us about his role in the community.
The combination of fiqh, wa'z, and tafsir shows scholarly versatility.
4
Consider the significance of his death
His sudden death is noted without judgment. Think about why Ibn Asakir mentions it was sudden and that he received the news from a trustworthy source.
Sudden death can be seen as a mercy or a reminder of mortality.
